Worked on a wearable infusion pump years ago, which was rated for rates down to 6 ul/h, which actually not as low as some insulin pumps go.  Even so, when measuring rates, we pumped to picogram resolution scales to get enough resolution/accuracy for our rate measurements.  At that scale, fingerprints on any part of the weighing setup would reek havoc on our measurements.  It wasnâ€™t so much that the set up was contaminated, it was that there were volatiles out gassing from the finger prints and they were also nucleation sites for condensation so the mass measurements would never stabilize.

We aren't "secretly on the metric system." Everything we buy and sell is measured in pounds, feet, gallons, ounces, square inches, and tons. That that makes us secretly on the metric system *is* a stupid idea.  The mass of a piece of metal is what it is whether you call it a kilogram or 2.2 lbs. It doesn't become metric or imperial when the engraver puts the markings on the balance like some weird quantum effect.

The most accurate kilogram was made by the university of Queensland in the late 80s. It was a sphere, so only one dimension vs the complexity of manufacturing a cylinder. The sphere was so round, if it was scaled up to the size of the earth its biggest peak to trough variation would be a few metres. They tried to get it adopted as the new standard but the French said no.

In 1572, Tycho Brahe noticed a completely new star that was brighter than any star or planet. Astonished by the existence of a star that ought not to have been there, he devoted himself to the creation of ever more accurate instruments of measurement over the next fifteen years (1576â€“1591). His trusty assistant was Johannes Kepler who knew the accuracy of the data, and in fact invented the "error bar" when explaining his Laws of Planetary Motion. Kepler improved on Galileo Galilei's optical telescope, and Galileo complimented Kepler's improvements. Isaac Newton said, "If I have seen farther than others, it is because I have stood on the shoulders of giants." Newton was thinking of Brahe, Kepler, and Galileo. All of this is to point out that both Newton's Law of Universal Gravitation, and (earlier) Kepler's Laws of Planetary Motion depended on improved measurements, just as Dr. Shaw says. Shaw is not justifying his work, he is stating a simple hard truth, that all of modern science and technology is standing on ever increasingly accurate measurements.
Let us be clear: defining the kilogram based on a Constant of Nature was an enormous triumph of science. It was the last of the Universal Weights and Measures to transition from an object to a Constant of Nature. Now we can tell the aliens around a distant star how much mass our bodies have, how tall our bodies are, and how long we live, and those aliens will get it absolutely correct.

It's important to note that the equation E=hf is specifically for massless particles like photons. The equation E=mc^2, on the other hand, applies to particles with mass that are at rest. These two equations should not be combined or used interchangeably.  The equation you derived, hf/c^2 = m, suggests that a photon has mass, which is not correct. Photons are massless particles that always travel at the speed of light in a vacuum. Their energy is determined by their frequency, as described by the equation E=hf. Because they are massless, photons do not have a rest mass, and the equation E=mc^2 does not apply to them.  It's essential to apply the appropriate equations and concepts depending on the situation and the type of particle being discussed. For massless particles like photons, the energy equation E=hf should be used, while for particles with mass that are at rest, the equation E=mc^2 is appropriate.

This, and a video of a guy taking a carpenter's level to a body of water, got me thinking about how what we think of as "perfectly straight" might actually be impossible to achieve.  Conceptually, if you make the carpenter's level long enough, and assume that it touches the surface of the water at every point on the level, then the level would be curved and that curve would match the curve of the earth.  A laser going off into space is the closest we can get to "perfectly straight," but even that would be affected by the warping of space time as it left the gravity well of the earth and as it passed by any body with significant mass.  If you shined a laser across a body of water, allowing it to touch at only one point, could you assume that it was the perfect tangent to the curve of the earth, or would earth's gravity be enough to measurably bend the path of the laser?

I worked in the semi-conductor realm and we used vacuum chambers (reactors) to grow our wafers.  This dude's lab seems to be on the same level however no one is in a bunny suit.  No one is wearing gloves or masks or glasses.  I didn't see them walk through an air shower.  It seems to me there would be enormous amounts of human to anything contamination in that lab.  Measuring to such a small degree seems like it would demand to be more careful about contamination than just using tweezers to pick up the standard weights.
I always used Al foil if I wanted to get fairly rough, but accurate low value weights of less than 1 gram. They usually show the thickness of the foil on the box, or you can measure it with a set of micrometers mine cost $5 from aliexpress and match up with my set of feeler gauges to within 3% down to 10 microns or 0.01 millimeter. So, knowing the thickness of the foil, and the density of aluminium, I can cut 1 square centimeter pieces (or smaller) with a razor and get pretty accurate weights. As accurate as I can cut them. It's a nice poor-man's method for making low value weights. Just be sure to take your errors in measuring/cutting them to size into account. Vernier calipers can help a lot to make accurate measurements for cutting.

I've order certified weights from NIST many times. As sample size increases the standard deviation of the means decreases. So if you're weighing something in the 10 milligram range uses you can't use just one below an one above the range, there are other standards in many industrys like ISO 9001 which require more and more data points, some times 5, I've even seen some that require 7 and your correlation coefficient to be greater then 0.998.  I'd be interested in knowing why the standards were getting lighter,  could be the mass lost on earth to solar winds?

I visited a lab that provides calibration services to, amongst others, the South African government (where I live). They procured a set of standard weights for a government department, verified that they were accurate and supplied them to said department. A couple of weeks later they received a complaint that the weights were inaccurate. They asked them to return the weights for inspection. My professor for metrology explained it quite nice: In a car, to get a good seal and work properly, the piston can have a (hypothetical) tolerance of 0.1mm. To make that part, the lathe it was made on must have a tolerance of 5um. To make that lathe, there is a special procedure and machine that can make lathes and has a tolerance of 0.1um. And to know that that machine and procedure is even working, you need a lab and scientist, that can measure down to 5nm. If you find a way to to that measurement down to 0.5nm (which might seem totally unnecessary), it's only a matter of time until the car will have a piston with 0.01mm tolerance (which can enable higher compression and better efficiency).

AFM cantilever tips are worth about $100 each and they are easily lost if the sample is irregular and they might crash on the surface. They are made from silicon nitride and the "diving board" is actually just the HOLDER for the tip, which is atomically pointed to have a surface area on the order of single digit nanometers. If you crashed the tip it's easy to see because the whole diving board will break off and be lost somewhere on your sample, again this is just the holder for the tip which probably requires SEM to view properly. Installing the tips onto the microscope is very tricky, easy to spoil them. They are $100 each so that is motivation to do it properly otherwise that money will be gone in seconds.

I work in the printer industry, actually designing ink jet print heads. A typical droplet has a volume of 3-4 pL (that is 3-4 * 1e-12 liters). We can measure flying droplets with a fancy high speed camera, using rediculously short laser pulses. So the volume of a droplet can be measured very exact. Now. We know the bulk weight of ink (or water for that matter). So we know the mass of a single droplet. This weight is way, way more smaller than a microgram. Such a droplet is of course not suitable to be used as a test weight. So: just for fun this comment.

Here's my Triplets Paradox, a situation which seems to defy time dilation theory. There are 3 objects in space moving along the same path, we'll call them objects 1, 2 and 3. The objects will always be the same distances from each other but those distances will become shorter as they move at their various velocities until they are eventually all together. The velocities are as follows: 1 is traveling toward 2 at a constant velocity, doesn't matter what it is for this exercise, it's just fast enough to make time dilation a noticeable factor. 2 is traveling toward 3 at the same velocity that 1 is traveling toward 2, and 3 is for our purposes stationary.   Here's what each of them see when they consider themselves to be stationary and the other two moving. 1 sees 2 coming toward it  and 3 coming toward it behind 2 at twice the velocity of 2. 3 sees the same thing as 1 saw but with the roles reversed, 2 coming toward it and 1 coming toward it at twice the velocity as 2. This part was simple enough, as regards time dilation.   Now comes the part which makes it a time dilation conundrum, or paradox. 2 sees something entirely different from what 1 and 3 saw, it sees both 1 and 3 coming toward it from opposite directions at the same velocities. That means it sees both 1 and 3 as having times which are equally behind his, meaning passing at a slower rate than his own time.   Objects 1 and 3 would not see the other two objects having equal time dilation factors relative to him, they would both see the closest one, object 2, having one time dilation factor and the farthest one having double that time dilation factor. The question is, do the three clocks show three different times or do two of the clocks show the same time and the other clock show a different time which is ahead of both of theirs?
This is how the double slit experiment actually works. Its a play off of Crookes Radiometer. The pressure of the laser causes the slit to resonate its like an opera singer singing the resonant frequency of a glass. The slits then resonate as expected and cause the photons to deflect off of the resonating blades, string etc.  And the reason why we prefer the imperial standard is that while metric is good for measurements, it's application would be retarded. Try tuning the electric grid to the metric scale. A foot, a minute, a year all have the same thing in common: easy of application, you can subdivide it by 1 2 3 4 or 5 in the case of 60. 12 can be subdivided by 1 2 3 or 4. This was understood since back in the days of sumer. How do we pretend to not know this?

For those wondering how we would calculate the force of a laser in theory, you just take the power of the laser and divide by the speed of light. For example a typical laser pointer might have a power of 1 to 5 milliwatts, which you divide by 3*10^8 m/s to get, indeed, a force in the piconewton range.
So tune a light beam to excite Cesium, measure its energy, convert that energy to mass, and multiply that mass by 156029346439284541350077670775371 1706058165632351/2351088797303619, and you've got a kilogram.

As an aspiring game dev I often think about the progress of science, and moreover what would be needed to build something like a computer from the ground up if you had all the knowledge but none of the tools. You can't just find some silica and copper, melt it and make a chip. There's tools to be created first, which allows for better and more precise technology, which allows for better tools again, and this goes back and forth a lot. Seeing this video really puts all of that into perspective. Not only as to how much work that goes into it, but especially the co-operation of both the different fields of technologies, and the effort and knowledge of so many people as well.

The Austrians are developing the thinnest wire in the world. When it was finished, they wanted to know its diameter. But since the wire is so thin, they can't measure it.  One of the Austrians said, "Let's send it to Germany. They are highly technologized. I'm sure they can measure it."  Says the other, "No, we won't take that chance. Let's send him to America instead. They are also highly developed."  So they put the wire in a box and enclose a note asking them to measure the diameter of the wire. After 2 weeks, the box comes back. A reply note says, "Unfortunately, we can't measure the diameter of the wire. Too thin."  On it the one Austrian says again: "Then we must send it to Germany."  Says the other, "No, we'd rather send it to China. Microchips are being built there. They can certainly measure it."  No sooner said than done. They enclose a note again, with the request to measure the diameter of the wire. After 2 weeks the box is back. The reply says, "Unfortunately, we can't measure this wire. Too thin."  Says the one Austrian: "Now, unfortunately, only Germany remains for us."  Says the other, "All right. Then we'll just send it to Germany."  So the wire is packed into a box again and sent to Germany. But this time the Austrians forget to enclose a note telling them what they actually want.  It takes 2 weeks, 3 weeks, 4 weeks ... no box. Only after 6 weeks the box comes again. And there is a note enclosed:  "We didn't know what to do with this wire. First we cut a thread on it and drilled a hole through it lengthwise. We hope that was in your sense!"

Technically, the force being measured here is the matter that is inside each photon being shot out of the laser. Proving yet again, in a long history of such proofs, that "energy" is just excited matter, in this case an electron attached to a dipole attached to an anti-electron without a nucleus aka a positron. Because there is matter in photons, the father away each galaxy is from Earth, the slower its photons travel as they sail through the Einstein-Bose Condensate that exists all through space. This makes the most distant galaxies seem to travel away from us faster and faster, creating the illusion of Doppler Red Shift.

I came across microcantilievers while doing my PhD bibilographic research on some pesticides traces sensing. Those are fabulous. You can also "cover" one side of the arm with specific molecules designed to react with the chemical that you want to sense. It then becomes sensitive to the desired molecule. Then the cavity below the arm (as shown in the big model in the video) is filled with the gas or the solution that you want to test. The sensitive arm then reacts with the analyte and that creates a bending force that is measured (like if you measured the bending force of a weight put onto the lever).  Despite being very hard to calibrate, this system can be 10^10 times more sensitive than the best sensing technologies ! So this technology was able to detect as low as 10000 molecules in one mL, which is nothing (there is 10^21 water molecules in one mL of water)! Its humongous sensitvity is also a huge drawback, so that you can't really take it out of the very controlled lab conditions. But the sensitivity of that thing is just breathtaking.

These types of electromagnetic balances have been used for decades in aerospace wind tunnels.  The general design and application was invented by Dr Fred Eastman at the University of Washington during WWII.  He never patented them, instead wanting this type of balance to be available for all research.

0:47 Yet another reason why the Imperial measurement system needs to be ditched. lol  And that's coming from a Brit, where we still cling on to imperial measurements, and I've had to use both for a lot of things. eg. We often still use stones, pounds, and ounces for things like body weight.  But I never fully got to grips with imperial anyway, as I always forget whether it's 12 or 14 pounds in a stone, etc. (it's 14 pounds in a stone.)  It was surprising to me when I was younger, to discover that the US still uses Imperial for so many things. I guess we do use MPH on motorways.  Imperial is also still used in PCB manufacture, but with a mixture of Metric as well. It's very strange, and confusing. (they use "mil" / "thou" a lot for things like drill hole diameters, trace widths and spacing.)  I only recently started measuring body weight in Kg. Once you get used to how it equates relative to what you're used to, it's fine. (a lot like using different currencies that you're not used to.)  And Metric is SO much easier to do calculations with vs Imperial. Everything in base-10 (or whatever the proper term is).  P.S. I genuinely have no idea how much a "quart" is. lol And then there are gallons vs litres, two different versions of "tonnes" vs "tons". sigh  "A ton is an imperial unit of mass equivalent to 1,016.047 kg or 2,240 lbs. A tonne is a metric unit of mass equivalent to 1,000 kg or 2,204.6 lbs."  The mix of units can cause huge problems. Not even the top scientists get it right sometimes. Like the space probe that crash-landed because they mixed up Imperial and Metric units. lol  And yes, I realize the irony that the Imperial system was first developed... in Britain. It's all our fault, sorry. lol

As an electrical engineer, we depend on our instruments to be traceable to a national standards laboratory.  Here in the US, that's NIST.  It's absolutely essential to make sure we measure the right thing and know we are measuring the right thing.  And for much of this sort of thing, NIST traceability isn't a one time thing - most instruments have to be periodically rechecked against NIST traceable instruments.  This, by the way, doesn't mean we send all our instruments to NIST every year.  What it means is somebody has a reference that may actually have gone to NIST (or one of the other labs that traceable to NIST, like Keysight, formerly Agilent, formerly HP).  So, its value is known compared to national standards.  That reference is then compared against our instruments.  Another cool thing NIST does is, you know those radio clocks (the ones sold as "Atomic Clocks")?  In the US, they get their time signal from NIST.  NIST has a radio station, WWVB, near Fort Collins, CO that broadcasts the signal those clocks use.

2:24 subdivision is one of the advantages of the imperial system. You can DIRECTLY go between units (pound / ounce, gallon / quart / cup / ounce, foot / inch) with subdivision. This means you can get whole subunits with nothing but a balance (IE 1 ounce of sugar from a pound of sugar). You CANNOT do this directly with a base 10 system. Not a big deal in today's modern world, but a huge pain in times gone by.

To add an extra point on the "stretching DNA" statement: in the lab, we use a technique called optical tweezer which is exactly what Dr Shaw said, using photons to move a bead that is tethered to a DNA strand or even protein. This allows us to have an extremely precise amount of force at the level of picoNewton that can stretch DNA or proteins out. We use this technique to understand the mechanical properties of biomolecules, as well as the spatial organisation of them. This is important because many diseases change protein and DNA's organisation and properties. Super cool.

I'm gonna make a tall claim here: There is no force-sensor. All sensors that claim to be force-sensors are, in reality, some form of displacement (or strain) sensors, or are counteractive precise actuators. The "force" is then only indirectly estimated. The stiffness of your cantilever, the d_33 of your piezo-crystal, the gage-factor of your strain-gage  or the transduction-constant of your kibble balance then is a "necessary evil" that must be an intermediary for _any_ force -measurement- estimation.

12:09 There's a small caveat to the force measuring of the laser pointer. It doesn't actually measure photonic impact pressure, but pressure differences due to heat absorption of the reflection, mostly. In order to measure true photonic pressure only, the measurement must be done in a vacuum, to avoid pressure differences, due to local heating of atmosphere, by the heat absorption of the laserlight.

Worth mentioning Sabine's argument that measuring extremely small forces could be a _huge_ deal in the coming decades if we can measure the force due to gravity from a body that's in a quantum superposition state. Somewhere between measuring ever smaller forces and getting ever larger masses in coherent superpositions, we might experimentally find out whether gravity acts quantum-mechanically, which is exciting no matter the answer.

That tiny weight is cool but people underestimate the danger of having something such a small weight. Did you notice the guy had a special orange hard hat on? That's a specific kind of PPE designed to stop tiny tiny objects from falling and piercing your skull. If a gust of wind or a sneeze event ever happened near that tiny weight it could be accelerated up to mach 5 in the blink of an eye and penetrate all the way through a human skull. Thus a special low mass hardhat must be worn at all times.

I actually have a funny story about NIST, not directly related to this video but I think it's worth sharing nonetheless. In a lab for my quantum mechanics class we were matching the emission line wavelengths from atomic light sources to the energy state transitions that caused them. To do this, we looked at NIST's values for the total energy of each atomic orbital and then found which wavelength emission corresponded to each gap between energy levels. My professor let us know that he would need to give us the energies of all Helium states with an n quantum number of 4, because they weren't available on NIST anymore. Apparently, and he swears this is true, the values used to be on the NIST website but when the government shut down in 2017, they temporarily shut down the website. When they brought it back, for whatever reason, these values for the energy states of Helium were missing. Thankfully my professor had these values recorded for himself, but he might be one of the few people out there who still has easy access to that obscure piece of information, which I find hilarious.

Question: When we reach such incredible precise depths in the range of Femtonewtons and beyond, doesn't our Environment influence the Measurement beyond Significance? I've actually never heard Metrologists talk about the Influences of Gravity, like the molten Mantels inside Earth shift and are in constant turmoil, some heavier Stream of Lava/Magma making some exact Point on top Earth's Surface a tiny bit heavier or lighter, or the speed of Earth's Rotation slowing down and speeding up, changing the Gravitational Constant.   Also also: as per Einstein's theory of Relativity, Gravity is not the only Factor. Sunlight hitting something outside vs no light inside excerpts a different Force. According to his Theory, a Flashlight turned on loses weight, however really really little (the mass of its Components divided by speed of light squared). On top of that, something measured at the top of Mount Everest has more potential Energy than something at Sea level, right? Which would then also Manifest as Mass or "Rest Mass" according to Relativity?  In Conclusion: Isn't the only place to make such fine Measurements in outer space, millions of light years away from any other Object's Sphere of Influence? Another reason to have this level of accuracy is if you are working with a torque wrench or a micrometer, things that have a certified accuracy those are calibrated and adjusted based on comparisons to standards like gauge blocks or torque transducers and those standards are calibrated using even more accurate equipment and again till you get to the international standards, and this system allows you to get a reasonable degree of confidence in your equipment without going all the way to nist, but still having the confidence that nist can provide through traceability. So if you have a piece of equipment that has a calibration certificate that can more than likely be traced from one standard to another till you get to the universal constants measured by NIST

Yes, have seen my local copy of the standard kilogram, it is only 10km away from me in it's armoured vault. Also have masspieces that are only 3 steps away from it, having been verified by a massmeter that was verified and calibrated with one of the sub masters compared to the local standard kilogram. 1g masspiece that is 1g with 4 zeros after it, any more and air pressure and bouyancy has an effect on it, which is why all those tiny measurements take place in hard vacuum.   The smaller masspices you cannot use in atmosphere, unless you measure your local air pressure and humidity, and compensate for the amount it bouys up the tiny masspiece, and, measuring that 100mg masspiece, simply moving your hand into view of the massmeter the IR radiation from your hand will make a change in the measurement.  Nice when the company coming in to do the verification uses your own masspices, simply because they have a fresher certificate, and they look at the long term drift and can see no trends, except for the one where it jumped up by 0.5g, which was because, a week before they went in, I took them stripped the paint off, and then gave them a new coat of primer and black, leaving a week in heat to flash off solvent. Yes also had the cotton gloves and wipe cloths, to make sure they stayed dust free and clean. The sub 200g ones had a separate box with the tweezers as well to handle them.

Not being on the metric system is not stupid. It doesn't make a difference. For every application in which the metric system is superior, practitioners already use metric. For the rest of the applications, it simply doesn't matter which you use, and so people use what they're used to, and that's perfectly fine. Miles or kilometers doesn't matter for 99% of normal human use cases. I've used both systems fluently in my lifetime and only in the lab do you ever really need the things metric does well (multiplication and division of units across several orders of magnitude).

For those wondering how we would calculate the force of a laser in theory, you just take the power of the laser and divide by the speed of light. For example a typical laser pointer might have a power of 1 to 5 milliwatts, which you divide by 3*10^8 m/s to get, indeed, a force in the piconewton range.

While I appreciate the trope, it's not stupid that most Americans operate from Imperial measurement. It's differently useful. Firstly, as stated, the US does, in fact, use metric. But many Americans know how to use both metric and Imperial. Imperial does have a couple interesting benefits going for it. For instance, when it comes to construction: Metric is easily divisible by 2, 5, and 10; Imperial is easily divided by 2, 3, 4, 6, and 12. The fact that two more easy dividers exist makes Imperial a bit more useful in construction.   Metric, in and of itself, is just made up, too. There isn't some form of measurement handed down from the Gods that is better or worse, inherently. Just different.

Using the imperial system is not stupid. The metric system is great for small units such as anything less than about 8 to 12 inches, but anything larger than that and the imperial system works better. I'm American and I regularly use the metric system for small measurements and I use the US imperial system for everything else.

This idea that the US is "actually metric" is the stupidest notion ever. You're going the wrong way. We adapted our units to SI for globalization. They are not "actually" different units. It's not even a pedantic argument, it's just wrong. Units are not "actually" what they are currently defined by; they are literally just an idea that represents a standard measurement. A foot is "actually" a foot regardless of what arbitrary method we use to reckon that length.
